									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/270px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" width="270" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/405px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/540px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="760" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">Watt steam engine</a>. <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a> transformed the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_motion" title="Reciprocating motion">reciprocating motion</a> that was used for pumping to a <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotating motion</a> suited to industrial applications. Watt and others significantly improved the efficiency of the steam engine.</div>
</div>

</table>
<p>The <b>Industrial Revolution</b> was the transition to new manufacturing processes in the period from about 1760 to sometime between 1820 and 1840. This transition included going from <a href="/wiki/Craft_production" title="Craft production">hand production methods</a> to <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a>, new <a href="/wiki/Chemical_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical manufacturing">chemical manufacturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron production">iron production</a> processes, improved efficiency of <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water power</a>, the increasing use of <a href="/wiki/Steam_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam power">steam power</a>, the development of <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a> and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Factory_system" title="Factory system">factory system</a>. <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">Textiles</a> were the dominant industry of the Industrial Revolution in terms of employment, value of output and <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> invested; the <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a> was also the first to use modern production methods.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_40_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_40-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Industrial Revolution began in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and most of the important <a href="/wiki/Technological_innovation" class="mw-redirect" title="Technological innovation">technological innovations</a> were British. Whereas <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolutism</a> stayed the normal form of power execution through most parts of Europe, institutions ensured <a href="/wiki/Property_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Property rights">property rights</a> and political safety to the people in the UK after the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688. Aided by these legal and cultural foundations, an <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurial" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneurial">entrepreneurial</a> spirit and <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumer</a> revolution drove <a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">industrialisation</a> in Britain, which would be emulated in countries around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Kiely_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiely-2">[2]</a></sup> A change in marrying patterns to getting married later made people able to accumulate more human capital during their youth, thereby encouraging economic development.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Industrial Revolution marks a major turning point in history; almost every aspect of daily life was influenced in some way. In particular, average income and population began to exhibit unprecedented sustained growth. Some economists say that the major impact of the Industrial Revolution was that the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> for the general population began to increase consistently for the first time in history, although others have said that it did not begin to meaningfully improve until the late 19th and 20th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Lectures_on_Economic_Growth_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lectures_on_Economic_Growth-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feinstein2014_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feinstein2014-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SzreterMooney2014_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SzreterMooney2014-6">[6]</a></sup> At approximately the same time the Industrial Revolution was occurring, Britain was undergoing an <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">agricultural revolution</a>, which also helped to improve living standards and provided surplus labour available for industry.</p>
<p>Mechanised textile production spread from Great Britain to continental Europe in the early 19th century, with important centres of textiles, iron and coal emerging in Belgium, and later in France. Since then industrialisation has spread throughout much of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_40_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_40-1">[1]</a></sup> The precise start and end of the Industrial Revolution is still debated among historians, as is the pace of economic and social changes.<sup id="cite_ref-revolution_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revolution-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rehabilitating_the_Industrial_Revolution_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rehabilitating_the_Industrial_Revolution-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lorenzen_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lorenzen-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GDP_per_capita" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP per capita">GDP per capita</a> was broadly stable before the Industrial Revolution and the emergence of the modern <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> economy,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Industrial_Revolution_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Industrial_Revolution-11">[11]</a></sup> while the Industrial Revolution began an era of per-capita <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> in capitalist economies.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Industrial_Revolution_.27.27Past_and_Future.27.27_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Industrial_Revolution_.27.27Past_and_Future.27.27-12">[12]</a></sup> Economic historians are in agreement that the onset of the Industrial Revolution is the most important event in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">history of humanity</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of animals and plants.<sup id="cite_ref-ReviewOfCambridge_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReviewOfCambridge-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The First Industrial Revolution evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">Second Industrial Revolution</a> in the transition years between 1840 and 1870, when technological and economic progress continued with the increasing adoption of steam transport (steam-powered railways, boats and ships), the large-scale manufacture of machine tools and the increasing use of machinery in steam-powered factories.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_1951_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_1951-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The earliest recorded use of the term "Industrial Revolution" seems to have been in a letter from 6 July 1799 written by French envoy <a href="/wiki/Louis-Guillaume_Otto" title="Louis-Guillaume Otto">Louis-Guillaume Otto</a>, announcing that France had entered the race to industrialise.<sup id="cite_ref-The_industrial_revolution_in_national_context:_Europe_and_the_USA_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_industrial_revolution_in_national_context:_Europe_and_the_USA-17">[17]</a></sup> In his 1976 book <i><a href="/wiki/Keywords:_A_Vocabulary_of_Culture_and_Society" title="Keywords: A Vocabulary of Culture and Society">Keywords: A Vocabulary of Culture and Society</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Williams" title="Raymond Williams">Raymond Williams</a> states in the entry for "Industry": "The idea of a new social order based on major industrial change was clear in <a href="/wiki/Robert_Southey" title="Robert Southey">Southey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Owen</a>, between 1811 and 1818, and was implicit as early as <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">Blake</a> in the early 1790s and <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a> at the turn of the [19th] century." The term <i>Industrial Revolution</i> applied to technological change was becoming more common by the late 1830s, as in <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me-Adolphe_Blanqui" title="Jérôme-Adolphe Blanqui">Jérôme-Adolphe Blanqui</a>'s description in 1837 of <i>la révolution industrielle</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Condition_of_the_Working_Class_in_England" title="The Condition of the Working Class in England">The Condition of the Working Class in England</a></i> in 1844 spoke of "an industrial revolution, a revolution which at the same time changed the whole of civil society". However, although Engels wrote in the 1840s, his book was not translated into English until the late 1800s, and his expression did not enter everyday language until then. Credit for popularising the term may be given to <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Toynbee" title="Arnold Toynbee">Arnold Toynbee</a>, whose 1881 lectures gave a detailed account of the term.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Industrial_Revolution13_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Industrial_Revolution13-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some historians, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Clapham_(economic_historian)" title="John Clapham (economic historian)">John Clapham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Crafts" title="Nicholas Crafts">Nicholas Crafts</a>, have argued that the economic and social changes occurred gradually and the term <i>revolution</i> is a misnomer. This is still a subject of debate among some historians.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_technological_developments">Important technological developments</span></h2>
<p>The commencement of the Industrial Revolution is closely linked to a small number of innovations,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Industrial_Revolution_.E2.80.93_Innovations_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Industrial_Revolution_.E2.80.93_Innovations-20">[20]</a></sup> beginning in the second half of the 18th century. By the 1830s the following gains had been made in important technologies:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Textiles</b> – mechanised cotton spinning powered by steam or water greatly increased the output of a worker. The <a href="/wiki/Power_loom" title="Power loom">power loom</a> increased the output of a worker by a factor of over 40.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a> increased productivity of removing seed from cotton by a factor of 50.<sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup> Large gains in productivity also occurred in spinning and weaving of wool and linen, but they were not as great as in cotton.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_00_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_00-22">[22]</a></sup>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg/180px-Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg" width="180" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg/270px-Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg/360px-Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3011" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Watt_James_von_Breda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a> improved on <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Newcomen" title="Thomas Newcomen">Newcomen</a>'s 1712 steam engine with his <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">Watt steam engine</a> in 1781</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li><b>Steam power</b> – the efficiency of <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> increased so that they used between one-fifth and one-tenth as much fuel. The adaptation of stationary steam engines to rotary motion made them suitable for industrial uses.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_82_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_82-23">[23]</a></sup> The high pressure engine had a high power to weight ratio, making it suitable for transportation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Steam power underwent a rapid expansion after 1800.</li>
<li><b>Iron making</b> – the substitution of <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> for charcoal greatly lowered the fuel cost for pig iron and wrought iron production.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Using coke also allowed larger blast furnaces,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> resulting in economies of scale. The cast iron blowing cylinder was first used in 1760. It was later improved by making it double acting, which allowed higher furnace temperatures. The <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddling process</a> produced a structural grade iron at a lower cost than the <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">finery forge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_91_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_91-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rolling_(metalworking)" title="Rolling (metalworking)">rolling mill</a> was fifteen times faster than hammering wrought iron. <a href="/wiki/Hot_blast" title="Hot blast">Hot blast</a> (1828) greatly increased fuel efficiency in iron production in the following decades.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><b>Invention of machine tools</b> - The first <a href="/wiki/Machine_tools" class="mw-redirect" title="Machine tools">machine tools</a> were invented. These included the <a href="/wiki/Screw_cutting_lathe" class="mw-redirect" title="Screw cutting lathe">screw cutting lathe</a>, cylinder boring machine and the <a href="/wiki/Milling_(machining)" title="Milling (machining)">milling machine</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textile_manufacture">Textile manufacture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution</a></div>
<p>In the late 17th and early 18th centuries the British government passed a series of <a href="/wiki/Calico_Acts" title="Calico Acts">Calico Acts</a> in order to <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protect</a> the domestic woollen industry from the increasing amounts of cotton fabric imported from its competitors in India.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_82_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_82-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ayers1989_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayers1989-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The demand for heavier fabric was met by a domestic industry based around <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a> that produced <a href="/wiki/Fustian" title="Fustian">fustian</a>, a cloth with flax <a href="/wiki/Warp_(weaving)" class="mw-redirect" title="Warp (weaving)">warp</a> and cotton <a href="/wiki/Weft" class="mw-redirect" title="Weft">weft</a>. Flax was used for the warp because wheel-spun cotton did not have sufficient strength, but the resulting blend was not as soft as 100% cotton and was more difficult to sew.<sup id="cite_ref-Ayers1989_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayers1989-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the eve of the Industrial Revolution, spinning and weaving were done in households, for domestic consumption and as a cottage industry under the <a href="/wiki/Putting-out_system" title="Putting-out system">putting-out system</a>. Occasionally the work was done in the workshop of a master weaver. Under the putting-out system, home-based workers produced under contract to merchant sellers, who often supplied the raw materials. In the off season the women, typically farmers' wives, did the spinning and the men did the weaving. Using the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_wheel" title="Spinning wheel">spinning wheel</a>, it took anywhere from four to eight spinners to supply one hand loom weaver.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_00_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_00-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ayers1989_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayers1989-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:823</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Flying_shuttle" title="Flying shuttle">flying shuttle</a> patented in 1733 by <a href="/wiki/John_Kay_(flying_shuttle)" title="John Kay (flying shuttle)">John Kay</a>, with a number of subsequent improvements including an important one in 1747, doubled the output of a weaver, worsening the imbalance between spinning and weaving. It became widely used around Lancashire after 1760 when John's son, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kay_(inventor)" title="Robert Kay (inventor)">Robert</a>, invented the drop box.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:821–22</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Paul" title="Lewis Paul">Lewis Paul</a> patented the roller <a href="/wiki/Spinning_frame" title="Spinning frame">spinning frame</a> and the flyer-and-bobbin system for drawing wool to a more even thickness. The technology was developed with the help of John Wyatt of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>. Paul and Wyatt opened a mill in Birmingham which used their new rolling machine powered by a donkey. In 1743 a factory opened in <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a> with 50 spindles on each of five of Paul and Wyatt's machines. This operated until about 1764. A similar mill was built by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bourn" title="Daniel Bourn">Daniel Bourn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Leominster" title="Leominster">Leominster</a>, but this burnt down. Both Lewis Paul and Daniel Bourn patented <a href="/wiki/Carding" title="Carding">carding</a> machines in 1748. Based on two sets of rollers that travelled at different speeds, it was later used in the first <a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">cotton spinning mill</a>. Lewis's invention was later developed and improved by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Arkwright" title="Richard Arkwright">Richard Arkwright</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Water_frame" title="Water frame">water frame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Crompton" title="Samuel Crompton">Samuel Crompton</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Spinning_mule" title="Spinning mule">spinning mule</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinning_jenny.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Spinning_jenny.jpg/230px-Spinning_jenny.jpg" width="230" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Spinning_jenny.jpg/345px-Spinning_jenny.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Spinning_jenny.jpg/460px-Spinning_jenny.jpg 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="582" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinning_jenny.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Model of the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_jenny" title="Spinning jenny">spinning jenny</a> in a museum in <a href="/wiki/Wuppertal" title="Wuppertal">Wuppertal</a>. Invented by <a href="/wiki/James_Hargreaves" title="James Hargreaves">James Hargreaves</a> in 1764, the spinning jenny was one of the innovations that started the revolution.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1764 in the village of Stanhill, Lancashire, <a href="/wiki/James_Hargreaves" title="James Hargreaves">James Hargreaves</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_jenny" title="Spinning jenny">spinning jenny</a>, which he patented in 1770. It was the first practical spinning frame with multiple spindles.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The jenny worked in a similar manner to the spinning wheel, by first clamping down on the fibres, then by drawing them out, followed by twisting.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It was a simple, wooden framed machine that only cost about £6 for a 40-spindle model in 1792,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and was used mainly by home spinners. The jenny produced a lightly twisted yarn only suitable for <a href="/wiki/Weft" class="mw-redirect" title="Weft">weft</a>, not <a href="/wiki/Warp_(weaving)" class="mw-redirect" title="Warp (weaving)">warp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:825–27</sup></p>
<p>The spinning frame or water frame was developed by Richard Arkwright who, along with two partners, patented it in 1769. The design was partly based on a spinning machine built for Thomas High by clockmaker John Kay, who was hired by Arkwright.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:827–30</sup> For each spindle, the water frame used a series of four pairs of rollers, each operating at a successively higher rotating speed, to draw out the fibre, which was then twisted by the spindle. The roller spacing was slightly longer than the fibre length. Too close a spacing caused the fibres to break while too distant a spacing caused uneven thread. The top rollers were leather-covered and loading on the rollers was applied by a weight. The weights kept the twist from backing up before the rollers. The bottom rollers were wood and metal, with fluting along the length. The water frame was able to produce a hard, medium count thread suitable for warp, finally allowing 100% cotton cloth to be made in Britain. A horse powered the first factory to use the spinning frame. Arkwright and his partners used water power at a factory in Cromford, Derbyshire in 1771, giving the invention its name.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mule-jenny.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Mule-jenny.jpg/220px-Mule-jenny.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Mule-jenny.jpg/330px-Mule-jenny.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Mule-jenny.jpg/440px-Mule-jenny.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="395" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mule-jenny.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The only surviving example of a <a href="/wiki/Spinning_mule" title="Spinning mule">spinning mule</a> built by the inventor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Crompton" title="Samuel Crompton">Samuel Crompton</a>. The mule produced superior quality thread with minimal labour.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Crompton" title="Samuel Crompton">Samuel Crompton</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Spinning_mule" title="Spinning mule">Spinning Mule</a>, introduced in 1779, was a combination of the spinning jenny and the water frame in which the spindles were placed on a carriage, which went through an operational sequence during which the rollers stopped while the carriage moved away from the drawing roller to finish drawing out the fibres as the spindles started rotating.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:832</sup> Crompton's mule was able to produce finer thread than hand spinning and at a lower cost. Mule spun thread was of suitable strength to be used as warp, and finally allowed Britain to produce good quality calico cloth.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:832</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marshall%27s_flax-mill,_Holbeck,_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg/220px-Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg/330px-Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg/440px-Marshall%27s_flax-mill%2C_Holbeck%2C_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1254" data-file-height="957" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marshall%27s_flax-mill,_Holbeck,_Leeds_-_interior_-_c.1800.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior of Marshall's <a href="/wiki/Temple_Works" title="Temple Works">Temple Works</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Realising that the expiration of the Arkwright patent would greatly increase the supply of spun cotton and lead to a shortage of weavers, Edmund Cartwright developed a vertical <a href="/wiki/Power_loom" title="Power loom">power loom</a> which he patented in 1785. In 1776 he patented a two-man operated loom which was more conventional.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:834</sup> Cartwright built two factories; the first burned down and the second was sabotaged by his workers. Cartwright's loom design had several flaws, the most serious being thread breakage. Samuel Horrocks patented a fairly successful loom in 1813. Horock's loom was improved by Richard Roberts in 1822 and these were produced in large numbers by Roberts, Hill &amp; Co.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The demand for cotton presented an opportunity to planters in the Southern United States, who thought upland cotton would be a profitable crop if a better way could be found to remove the seed. <a href="/wiki/Eli_Whitney" title="Eli Whitney">Eli Whitney</a> responded to the challenge by inventing the inexpensive <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a>. With a cotton gin a man could remove seed from as much upland cotton in one day as would have previously taken a woman working two months to process at one pound per day.<sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other inventors increased the efficiency of the individual steps of spinning (carding, twisting and spinning, and rolling) so that the supply of <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarn</a> increased greatly. This in turn fed a weaving industry that advanced with improvements to <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_(weaving)" title="Shuttle (weaving)">shuttles</a> and the loom or 'frame'. The output of an individual labourer increased dramatically, with the effect that the new machines threatened the bargaining power of the labourers, and early innovators were attacked and their inventions destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>These advances were capitalised on by <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneurs</a>, of whom the best known is Richard Arkwright. He is credited with a list of inventions, but these were actually developed by such people as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Highs" title="Thomas Highs">Thomas Highs</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Kay_(spinning_frame)" title="John Kay (spinning frame)">John Kay</a>; Arkwright nurtured the inventors, patented the ideas, financed the initiatives, and protected the machines. He created the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">cotton mill</a> which brought the production processes together in a factory, and he developed the use of power—first horse power and then <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">water power</a>—which made cotton manufacture a mechanised industry. Before long <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">steam power</a> was applied to drive textile machinery. <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> acquired the nickname <a href="/wiki/Cottonopolis" title="Cottonopolis">Cottonopolis</a> during the early 19th century owing to its sprawl of textile factories.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png/220px-Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png/330px-Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png 2x" data-file-width="351" data-file-height="224" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reverberatory_furnace_diagram.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Reverberatory_furnace" title="Reverberatory furnace">reverberatory furnace</a> could produce <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> using mined coal. The burning coal remained separate from the iron ore and so did not contaminate the iron with impurities like sulphur and ash. This opened the way to increased iron production.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ironbridge_6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/220px-Ironbridge_6.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/330px-Ironbridge_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Ironbridge_6.jpg/440px-Ironbridge_6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2216" data-file-height="1648" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ironbridge_6.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">The Iron Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a>, England</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A major change in the metal industries during the era of the Industrial Revolution was the replacement of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and other bio-fuels with coal. For a given amount of heat, coal required much less labour to mine than cutting wood and converting it to charcoal,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and coal was more abundant than wood.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_00_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_00-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Use of coal in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> started somewhat before the Industrial Revolution, based on innovations by Sir <a href="/wiki/Clement_Clerke" title="Clement Clerke">Clement Clerke</a> and others from 1678, using coal <a href="/wiki/Reverberatory_furnace" title="Reverberatory furnace">reverberatory furnaces</a> known as cupolas. These were operated by the flames playing on the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> and charcoal or <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> mixture, <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> the <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> to metal. This has the advantage that impurities (such as sulphur ash) in the coal do not migrate into the metal. This technology was applied to <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> from 1678 and to <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> from 1687. It was also applied to iron foundry work in the 1690s, but in this case the reverberatory furnace was known as an air furnace. (The <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">foundry cupola</a> is a different (and later) innovation.)</p>
<p>This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby</a>, who made great strides using coke to fuel his <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a> at <a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale" title="Coalbrookdale">Coalbrookdale</a> in 1709. However, the coke <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> he made was used mostly for the production of <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> goods, such as pots and kettles. He had the advantage over his rivals in that his pots, cast by his patented process, were thinner and cheaper than theirs. Coke pig iron was hardly used to produce <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> in forges until the mid-1750s, when his son <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_II" title="Abraham Darby II">Abraham Darby II</a> built <a href="/wiki/Horsehay" title="Horsehay">Horsehay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ketley" title="Ketley">Ketley</a> furnaces (not far from Coalbrookdale). By then, coke pig iron was cheaper than charcoal pig iron. Since cast iron was becoming cheaper and more plentiful, it began being a structural material following the building of the innovative <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">Iron Bridge</a> in 1778 by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_III" title="Abraham Darby III">Abraham Darby III</a>.</p>
<p>Wrought iron for smiths to forge into consumer goods was still made in <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">finery forges</a>, as it long had been. However, new processes were adopted in the ensuing years. The first is referred to today as <a href="/wiki/Potting_and_stamping" title="Potting and stamping">potting and stamping</a>, but this was superseded by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cort" title="Henry Cort">Henry Cort</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Puddling_furnace" class="mw-redirect" title="Puddling furnace">puddling</a> process. Cort developed two significant iron manufacturing processes: <a href="/wiki/Rolling_(metalworking)" title="Rolling (metalworking)">rolling</a> in 1783 and <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddling</a> in 1784.<sup id="cite_ref-David_S._Landes_1969_91_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_S._Landes_1969_91-28">[28]</a></sup> Rolling replaced hammering for consolidating wrought iron and expelling some of the dross. Rolling was 15 times faster than hammering with a trip hammer. Roller mills were first used for making sheets, but also were developed for rolling structural shapes such as angles and rails.</p>
<p>Puddling produced a structural grade iron at a relatively low cost. Puddling was a means of decarburizing pig iron by slow oxidation, with iron ore as the oxygen source, as the iron was manually stirred using a long rod. The decarburized iron, having a higher melting point than cast iron, was raked into globs by the puddler. When the glob was large enough the puddler would remove it. Puddling was backbreaking and extremely hot work. Few puddlers lived to be 40.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Puddling was done in a reverberatory furnace, allowing coal or coke to be used as fuel. The puddling process continued to be used until the late 19th century when iron was being displaced by steel. Because puddling required human skill in sensing the iron globs, it was never successfully mechanised.</p>
<p>Up to that time, British iron manufacturers had used considerable amounts of imported iron to supplement native supplies. This came principally from Sweden from the mid-17th century and later also from Russia from the end of the 1720s. However, from 1785, imports decreased because of the new iron making technology, and Britain became an exporter of bar iron as well as manufactured wrought iron <a href="/wiki/Final_good" title="Final good">consumer goods</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hot_blast" title="Hot blast">Hot blast</a>, patented by <a href="/wiki/James_Beaumont_Neilson" title="James Beaumont Neilson">James Beaumont Neilson</a> in 1828, was the most important development of the 19th century for saving energy in making pig iron. By using waste exhaust heat to preheat combustion air, the amount of fuel to make a unit of pig iron was reduced at first by between one-third using coal or two-thirds using coke;<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> however, the efficiency gains continued as the technology improved.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Hot blast also raised the operating temperature of furnaces, increasing their capacity. Using less coal or coke meant introducing fewer impurities into the pig iron. This meant that lower quality coal or <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a> could be used in areas where coking coal was unavailable or too expensive;<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> however, by the end of the 19th century transportation costs fell considerably.</p>
<p>Two decades before the Industrial Revolution an improvement was made in the production of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, which was an expensive commodity and used only where iron would not do, such as for cutting edge tools and for springs. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Huntsman" title="Benjamin Huntsman">Benjamin Huntsman</a> developed his <a href="/wiki/Crucible_steel" title="Crucible steel">crucible steel</a> technique in the 1740s. The raw material for this was blister steel, made by the <a href="/wiki/Cementation_process" title="Cementation process">cementation process</a>.</p>
<p>The supply of cheaper iron and steel aided a number of industries, such as those making nails, hinges, wire and other hardware items. The development of <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a> allowed better working of iron, causing it to be increasingly used in the rapidly growing machinery and engine industries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_power">Steam power</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steam_power_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" title="Steam power during the Industrial Revolution">Steam power during the Industrial Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Savery-engine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg/170px-Savery-engine.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg/255px-Savery-engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="426" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Savery-engine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1698 <i>Savery Engine</i> (piston-less steam pump) – the world's first commercially useful steam-powered device: built by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Savery" title="Thomas Savery">Thomas Savery</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Stationary_steam_engine" title="Stationary steam engine">stationary steam engine</a> was an important element of the Industrial Revolution; however, during the early period of the Industrial Revolution, the majority of industrial power was supplied by water and wind. In Britain by 1800 an estimated 10,000 horsepower was being supplied by steam. By 1815 steam power had grown to 210,000&#160;hp.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Small industrial power requirements continued to be provided by animal and human muscle until widespread electrification in the early 20th century. These included <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank</a>-, <a href="/wiki/Treadle" title="Treadle">treadle</a>-powered and horse-powered workshop and light industrial machinery.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first commercially successful industrial use of steam power was due to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Savery" title="Thomas Savery">Thomas Savery</a> in 1698. He constructed and patented in London a low-lift combined vacuum and pressure water pump, that generated about one <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower</a> (hp) and was used in numerous water works and in a few mines (hence its "brand name", <i>The Miner's Friend</i>). Savery's pump was economical in small horsepower ranges, but was prone to boiler explosions in larger sizes. Savery pumps continued to be produced until the late 18th century.</p>
<p>The first successful piston steam engine was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Newcomen" title="Thomas Newcomen">Thomas Newcomen</a> before 1712. A number of Newcomen engines were successfully put to use in Britain for draining hitherto unworkable deep mines, with the engine on the surface; these were large machines, requiring a lot of capital to build, and produced about 5&#160;hp (3.7&#160;kW). They were extremely inefficient by modern standards, but when located where coal was cheap at pit heads, opened up a great expansion in coal mining by allowing mines to go deeper. Despite their disadvantages, Newcomen engines were reliable and easy to maintain and continued to be used in the coalfields until the early decades of the 19th century. By 1729, when Newcomen died, his engines had spread (first) to Hungary in 1722, Germany, Austria, and Sweden. A total of 110 are known to have been built by 1733 when the joint patent expired, of which 14 were abroad. In the 1770s the engineer <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a> built some very large examples and introduced a number of improvements. A total of 1,454 engines had been built by 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-allen_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allen-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png/170px-Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png/255px-Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png/340px-Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png 2x" data-file-width="366" data-file-height="446" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newcomens_Dampfmaschine_aus_Meyers_1890.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Newcomen_steam_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcomen steam engine">Newcomen's steam-powered atmospheric engine</a> was the first practical piston steam engine. Subsequent steam engines were to power the Industrial Revolution.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A fundamental change in working principles was brought about by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scotsman</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>. In close collaboration with <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">Englishman</a> <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">Matthew Boulton</a>, he had succeeded by 1778 in perfecting his <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">steam engine</a>, which incorporated a series of radical improvements, notably the closing off of the upper part of the cylinder, thereby making the low-pressure steam drive the top of the piston instead of the atmosphere, use of a steam jacket and the celebrated separate steam condenser chamber. The separate condenser did away with the cooling water that had been injected directly into the cylinder, which cooled the cylinder and wasted steam. Likewise, the steam jacket kept steam from condensing in the cylinder, also improving efficiency. These improvements increased engine efficiency so that Boulton &amp; Watts engines used only 20–25% as much coal per horsepower-hour as Newcomen's. Boulton and Watt opened the <a href="/wiki/Soho_Foundry" title="Soho Foundry">Soho Foundry</a> for the manufacture of such engines in 1795.</p>
<p>By 1783 the Watt steam engine had been fully developed into a <a href="/wiki/Single-_and_double-acting_cylinders" title="Single- and double-acting cylinders">double-acting</a> rotative type, which meant that it could be used to directly drive the rotary machinery of a factory or mill. Both of Watt's basic engine types were commercially very successful, and by 1800, the firm <a href="/wiki/Boulton_and_Watt" title="Boulton and Watt">Boulton &amp; Watt</a> had constructed 496 engines, with 164 driving reciprocating pumps, 24 serving <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>, and 308 powering mill machinery; most of the engines generated from 5 to 10&#160;hp (3.7 to 7.5&#160;kW).</p>
<p>The development of <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a>, such as the engine <a href="/wiki/Lathe" title="Lathe">lathe</a>, planing, milling and shaping machines powered by these engines, enabled all the metal parts of the engines to be easily and accurately cut and in turn made it possible to build larger and more powerful engines.</p>
<p>Until about 1800 the most common pattern of steam engine was the <a href="/wiki/Beam_engine" title="Beam engine">beam engine</a>, built as an integral part of a stone or brick engine-house, but soon various patterns of self-contained rotative engines (readily removable, but not on wheels) were developed, such as the <a href="/wiki/Table_engine" title="Table engine">table engine</a>. Around the start of the 19th century, the Cornish engineer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a> and the American <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Evans" title="Oliver Evans">Oliver Evans</a> began to construct higher-pressure non-condensing steam engines, exhausting against the atmosphere. High pressure yielded an engine and boiler compact enough to be used on mobile road and rail <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steam boats</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Machine_tools">Machine tools</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">Machine tool</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Interchangeable_parts" title="Interchangeable parts">Interchangeable parts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png/220px-Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png/330px-Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png/440px-Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png 2x" data-file-width="523" data-file-height="749" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maudslay_screw-cutting_lathes_of_circa_1797_and_1800.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maudslay's famous early screw-cutting lathes of circa 1797 and 1800</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png/220px-Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png" width="220" height="62" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png/330px-Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png/440px-Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png 2x" data-file-width="690" data-file-height="195" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middletown_milling_machine_1818--001.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Middletown <a href="/wiki/Milling_(machining)" title="Milling (machining)">milling machine</a> of circa 1818, associated with Robert Johnson and Simeon North</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Industrial Revolution created a demand for metal parts used in machinery. This led to the development of several machine tools for cutting metal parts. They have their origins in the tools developed in the 18th century by makers of clocks and watches and scientific instrument makers to enable them to batch-produce small mechanisms.</p>
<p>Before the advent of machine tools, metal was worked manually using the basic hand tools of hammers, files, scrapers, saws and chisels. Consequently, the use of metal was kept to a minimum. Wooden components had the disadvantage of changing dimensions with temperature and humidity, and the various joints tended to rack (work loose) over time. As the Industrial Revolution progressed, machines with metal parts and frames became more common. Hand methods of production were very laborious and costly and precision was difficult to achieve. Pre-industrial machinery was built by various craftsmen—<a href="/wiki/Millwright" title="Millwright">millwrights</a> built water and wind mills, <a href="/wiki/Carpenter" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpenter">carpenters</a> made wooden framing, and smiths and turners made metal parts.</p>
<p>The first large machine tool was the cylinder <a href="/wiki/Boring_(manufacturing)" title="Boring (manufacturing)">boring machine</a> used for boring the large-diameter cylinders on early steam engines. The <a href="/wiki/Planer_(metalworking)" title="Planer (metalworking)">planing machine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Milling_(machining)" title="Milling (machining)">milling machine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shaper" title="Shaper">shaping machine</a> were developed in the early decades of the 19th century. Although the milling machine was invented at this time, it was not developed as a serious workshop tool until somewhat later in the 19th century.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_Maudslay" title="Henry Maudslay">Henry Maudslay</a>, who trained a school of machine tool makers early in the 19th century, was a mechanic with superior ability who had been employed at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Arsenal" title="Royal Arsenal">Royal Arsenal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woolwich" title="Woolwich">Woolwich</a>. He was hired away by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bramah" title="Joseph Bramah">Joseph Bramah</a> for the production of high security metal locks that required precision craftsmanship. Bramah patented a lathe that had similarities to the slide rest lathe. Maudslay perfected the slide rest lathe, which could cut machine screws of different thread pitches by using changeable gears between the spindle and the lead screw. Before its invention screws could not be cut to any precision using various earlier lathe designs, some of which copied from a template.<sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McNeil1990_30-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil1990-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:392–95</sup> The slide rest lathe was called one of history's most important inventions, although not entirely Maudslay's idea.<sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31, 36</sup></p>
<p>Maudslay left Bramah's employment and set up his own shop. He was engaged to build the machinery for making ships' pulley blocks for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Block_Mills" title="Portsmouth Block Mills">Portsmouth Block Mills</a>. These machines were all-metal and were the first machines for <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> and making components with a degree of <a href="/wiki/Interchangeable_parts" title="Interchangeable parts">interchangeability</a>. The lessons Maudslay learned about the need for stability and precision he adapted to the development of machine tools, and in his workshops he trained a generation of men to build on his work, such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Roberts_(engineer)" title="Richard Roberts (engineer)">Richard Roberts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Clement" title="Joseph Clement">Joseph Clement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Whitworth" title="Joseph Whitworth">Joseph Whitworth</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Fox_(engineer)" title="James Fox (engineer)">James Fox</a> of <a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a> had a healthy export trade in machine tools for the first third of the century, as did <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Murray" title="Matthew Murray">Matthew Murray</a> of Leeds. Roberts was a maker of high-quality machine tools and a pioneer of the use of jigs and gauges for precision workshop measurement.</p>
<p>The impact of machine tools during the Industrial Revolution was not that great because other than firearms, threaded fasteners and a few other industries there were few mass-produced metal parts. The techniques to make mass produced metal parts made with sufficient precision to be <a href="/wiki/Interchangeable_parts" title="Interchangeable parts">interchangeable</a> is largely attributed to a program of the U.S. Department of War which perfected <a href="/wiki/Interchangeable_parts" title="Interchangeable parts">interchangeable parts</a> for firearms in the early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Hounshell-1984_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hounshell-1984-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the half century following the invention of the fundamental machine tools the <a href="/wiki/Machine_industry" title="Machine industry">machine industry</a> became the largest industrial sector of the economy, by value added, in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-faculty.wcas.northwestern.edu_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faculty.wcas.northwestern.edu-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals">Chemicals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Humphry_Davy,_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/180px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg" width="180" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/270px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg/360px-Sir_Humphry_Davy%2C_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3123" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Humphry_Davy,_Bt_by_Thomas_Phillips.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The large scale production of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemicals</a> was an important development during the Industrial Revolution. The first of these was the production of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulphuric acid</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a> invented by the Englishman <a href="/wiki/John_Roebuck" title="John Roebuck">John Roebuck</a> (<a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>'s first partner) in 1746. He was able to greatly increase the scale of the manufacture by replacing the relatively expensive glass vessels formerly used with larger, less expensive chambers made of <a href="/wiki/Rivet" title="Rivet">riveted</a> sheets of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. Instead of making a small amount each time, he was able to make around 100 pounds (50&#160;kg) in each of the chambers, at least a tenfold increase.</p>
<p>The production of an <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> on a large scale became an important goal as well, and <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Leblanc" title="Nicolas Leblanc">Nicolas Leblanc</a> succeeded in 1791 in introducing a method for the production of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Leblanc_process" title="Leblanc process">Leblanc process</a> was a reaction of sulphuric acid with sodium chloride to give sodium sulphate and <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sodium_sulfate" title="Sodium sulfate">sodium sulphate</a> was heated with <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>) and coal to give a mixture of sodium carbonate and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfide" title="Calcium sulfide">calcium sulphide</a>. Adding water separated the soluble sodium carbonate from the calcium sulphide. The process produced a large amount of pollution (the hydrochloric acid was initially vented to the air, and calcium sulphide was a useless waste product). Nonetheless, this synthetic <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">soda ash</a> proved economical compared to that from burning specific plants (<a href="/wiki/Barilla" title="Barilla">barilla</a>) or from <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>, which were the previously dominant sources of soda ash,<sup id="cite_ref-Clow52_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clow52-47">[47]</a></sup> and also to <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a> (<a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a>) derived from hardwood ashes.</p>
<p>These two chemicals were very important because they enabled the introduction of a host of other inventions, replacing many small-scale operations with more cost-effective and controllable processes. Sodium carbonate had many uses in the glass, textile, soap, and <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> industries. Early uses for sulphuric acid included pickling (removing rust) iron and steel, and for <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleaching</a> cloth.</p>
<p>The development of bleaching powder (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_hypochlorite" title="Calcium hypochlorite">calcium hypochlorite</a>) by Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Tennant" title="Charles Tennant">Charles Tennant</a> in about 1800, based on the discoveries of French chemist <a href="/wiki/Claude_Louis_Berthollet" title="Claude Louis Berthollet">Claude Louis Berthollet</a>, revolutionised the bleaching processes in the textile industry by dramatically reducing the time required (from months to days) for the traditional process then in use, which required repeated exposure to the sun in bleach fields after soaking the textiles with alkali or sour milk. Tennant's factory at St Rollox, North <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, became the largest chemical plant in the world.</p>
<p>After 1860 the focus on chemical innovation was in <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyestuffs</a>, and Germany took world leadership, building a strong chemical industry.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Aspiring chemists flocked to German universities in the 1860–1914 era to learn the latest techniques. British scientists by contrast, lacked research universities and did not train advanced students; instead the practice was to hire German-trained chemists.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cement">Cement</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thamestunnel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thamestunnel.jpg/220px-Thamestunnel.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thamestunnel.jpg/330px-Thamestunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Thamestunnel.jpg/440px-Thamestunnel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="318" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thamestunnel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Thames_Tunnel" title="Thames Tunnel">Thames Tunnel</a> (opened 1843).<br />
<a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">Cement</a> was used in the world's first underwater tunnel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1824 <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Aspdin" title="Joseph Aspdin">Joseph Aspdin</a>, a British <a href="/wiki/Bricklayer" title="Bricklayer">bricklayer</a> turned builder, patented a chemical process for making <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">portland cement</a> which was an important advance in the building trades. This process involves <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintering</a> a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> to about 1,400&#160;°C (2,552&#160;°F), then <a href="/wiki/Grinding_(abrasive_cutting)" title="Grinding (abrasive cutting)">grinding</a> it into a fine powder which is then mixed with water, sand and <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>. Portland cement was used by the famous English engineer <a href="/wiki/Marc_Isambard_Brunel" title="Marc Isambard Brunel">Marc Isambard Brunel</a> several years later when constructing the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Tunnel" title="Thames Tunnel">Thames Tunnel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-memphis_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memphis-50">[50]</a></sup> Cement was used on a large scale in the construction of the <a href="/wiki/London_sewerage_system" title="London sewerage system">London sewerage system</a> a generation later.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_lighting">Gas lighting</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gas_lighting" title="Gas lighting">Gas lighting</a></div>
<p>Another major industry of the later Industrial Revolution was <a href="/wiki/Gas_lighting" title="Gas lighting">gas lighting</a>. Though others made a similar innovation elsewhere, the large-scale introduction of this was the work of <a href="/wiki/William_Murdoch" title="William Murdoch">William Murdoch</a>, an employee of Boulton &amp; Watt, the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">steam engine</a> pioneers. The process consisted of the large-scale gasification of coal in furnaces, the purification of the gas (removal of sulphur, ammonia, and heavy hydrocarbons), and its storage and distribution. The first gas lighting utilities were established in London between 1812 and 1820. They soon became one of the major consumers of coal in the UK. Gas lighting affected social and industrial organisation because it allowed factories and stores to remain open longer than with tallow candles or oil. Its introduction allowed nightlife to flourish in cities and towns as interiors and streets could be lighted on a larger scale than before.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glass_making">Glass making</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glass_production" title="Glass production">Glass production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg/220px-Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg/330px-Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg/440px-Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1611" data-file-height="1095" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_Palace_interior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Crystal Palace held the <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" title="The Great Exhibition">Great Exhibition</a> of 1851</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A new method of producing glass, known as the <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_blown_sheet_glass" title="Cylinder blown sheet glass">cylinder process</a>, was developed in Europe during the early 19th century. In 1832 this process was used by the <a href="/wiki/Chance_Brothers" title="Chance Brothers">Chance Brothers</a> to create sheet glass. They became the leading producers of window and plate glass. This advancement allowed for larger panes of glass to be created without interruption, thus freeing up the space planning in interiors as well as the fenestration of buildings. <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a> is the supreme example of the use of sheet glass in a new and innovative structure.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper_machine">Paper machine</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paper_machine" title="Paper machine">Paper machine</a></div>
<p>A machine for making a continuous sheet of paper on a loop of wire fabric was patented in 1798 by Nicholas Louis Robert who worked for <a href="/wiki/Saint-L%C3%A9ger_Didot" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint-Léger Didot">Saint-Léger Didot</a> family in France. The paper machine is known as a Fourdrinier after the financiers, brothers Sealy and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fourdrinier" title="Henry Fourdrinier">Henry Fourdrinier</a>, who were <a href="/wiki/Stationers" class="mw-redirect" title="Stationers">stationers</a> in London. Although greatly improved and with many variations, the Fourdriner machine is the predominant means of paper production today.</p>
<p>The method of <a href="/wiki/Continuous_production" title="Continuous production">continuous production</a> demonstrated by the paper machine influenced the development of continuous rolling of iron and later steel and other <a href="/wiki/Continuous_production" title="Continuous production">continuous production</a> processes.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a> is considered one of the causes of the Industrial Revolution because improved agricultural productivity freed up workers to work in other sectors of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Industrial technologies that affected farming included the <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drill</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution#The_Dutch_and_Rotherham_swing_.28wheel-less.29_plough" title="British Agricultural Revolution">Dutch plough</a>, which contained iron parts, and the threshing machine.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jethro_Tull_(agriculturist)" title="Jethro Tull (agriculturist)">Jethro Tull</a> invented an improved seed drill in 1701. It was a mechanical seeder which distributed seeds evenly across a plot of land and planted them at the correct depth. This was important because the yield of seeds harvested to seeds planted at that time was around four or five. Tull's seed drill was very expensive and not very reliable and therefore did not have much of an impact. Good quality seed drills were not produced until the mid 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Joseph Foljambe's <i>Rotherham <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plough</a></i> of 1730, was the first commercially successful iron plough.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Threshing_machine" title="Threshing machine">threshing machine</a>, invented by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Meikle" title="Andrew Meikle">Andrew Meikle</a> in 1784, displaced hand threshing with a <a href="/wiki/Flail" title="Flail">flail</a>, a laborious job that took about one-quarter of agricultural labour.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark2007_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2007-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:286</sup> It took several decades to diffuse<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and was the final straw for many farm labourers, who faced near starvation, leading to the 1830 agricultural rebellion of the <a href="/wiki/Swing_Riots" title="Swing Riots">Swing Riots</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Machine_tools" class="mw-redirect" title="Machine tools">Machine tools</a> and metalworking techniques developed during the Industrial Revolution eventually resulted in precision manufacturing techniques in the late 19th century for mass-producing agricultural equipment, such as reapers, binders and combine harvesters.<sup id="cite_ref-Hounshell-1984_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hounshell-1984-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg/220px-Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg/330px-Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg/440px-Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg 2x" data-file-width="495" data-file-height="325" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middleton_Colliery_1814.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Middleton miner in 1814</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">Coal mining</a> in Britain, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Wales" title="Economy of Wales">South Wales</a> started early. Before the steam engine, <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">pits</a> were often shallow <a href="/wiki/Bell_pit" title="Bell pit">bell pits</a> following a seam of coal along the surface, which were abandoned as the coal was extracted. In other cases, if the geology was favourable, the coal was mined by means of an <a href="/wiki/Adit" title="Adit">adit</a> or <a href="/wiki/Drift_mining" title="Drift mining">drift mine</a> driven into the side of a hill. <a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">Shaft mining</a> was done in some areas, but the limiting factor was the problem of removing water. It could be done by hauling buckets of water up the shaft or to a <a href="/wiki/Sough" title="Sough">sough</a> (a tunnel driven into a hill to drain a mine). In either case, the water had to be discharged into a stream or ditch at a level where it could flow away by gravity. The introduction of the steam pump by <a href="/wiki/Savery" title="Savery">Savery</a> in 1698 and the <a href="/wiki/Newcomen_steam_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcomen steam engine">Newcomen steam engine</a> in 1712 greatly facilitated the removal of water and enabled shafts to be made deeper, enabling more coal to be extracted. These were developments that had begun before the Industrial Revolution, but the adoption of <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a>'s improvements to the Newcomen engine followed by James Watt's more efficient steam engines from the 1770s reduced the fuel costs of engines, making mines more profitable.</p>
<p>Coal mining was very dangerous owing to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Firedamp" title="Firedamp">firedamp</a> in many coal seams. Some degree of safety was provided by the <a href="/wiki/Safety_lamp" title="Safety lamp">safety lamp</a> which was invented in 1816 by Sir <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> and independently by <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a>. However, the lamps proved a false dawn because they became unsafe very quickly and provided a weak light. Firedamp explosions continued, often setting off <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a> <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosions</a>, so casualties grew during the entire 19th century. Conditions of work were very poor, with a high casualty rate from rock falls.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_developments">Other developments</span></h3>
<p>Other developments included more efficient <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheels</a>, based on experiments conducted by the British engineer <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> the beginnings of a machine industry<sup id="cite_ref-Roe1916_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roe1916-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and the rediscovery of <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> (based on <a href="/wiki/Lime_mortar" title="Lime mortar">hydraulic lime mortar</a>) by <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a>, which had been lost for 1300 years.<sup id="cite_ref-reintroduction_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reintroduction-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_during_the_British_Industrial_Revolution" title="Transport during the British Industrial Revolution">Transport during the British Industrial Revolution</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Productivity_improving_technologies_(economic_history)#Infrastructures" class="mw-redirect" title="Productivity improving technologies (economic history)">Productivity improving technologies (economic history) §&#160;Infrastructures</a></div>
<p>At the beginning of the Industrial Revolution, inland transport was by navigable rivers and roads, with coastal vessels employed to move heavy goods by sea. Wagon ways were used for conveying coal to rivers for further shipment, but canals had not yet been widely constructed. Animals supplied all of the motive power on land, with sails providing the motive power on the sea. The first horse railways were introduced toward the end of the 18th century, with steam locomotives being introduced in the early decades of the 19th century.</p>
<p>The Industrial Revolution improved Britain's transport infrastructure with a turnpike road network, a canal and waterway network, and a railway network. Raw materials and finished products could be moved more quickly and cheaply than before. Improved transportation also allowed new ideas to spread quickly.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canals">Canals</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_canal_system" title="History of the British canal system">History of the British canal system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
